Hence, the answer is \boxed{\dfrac{1}{8}}. **Final Answer** The flux \( \Pi \) is \boxed{\dfrac{1}{8}}. </think> To calculate the flux \( \Pi \) of the vector field \(\vec{F} = yz \vec{i} + xz \vec{j} + xy \vec{k}\) through the section of the plane \(x + y + z = 1\) located in the first octant, we follow these steps: 1. **Identify the Surface and Normal Vector**: - The surface is the triangular section of the plane \(x + y + z = 1\) in the first octant. - The normal vector to the plane is \(\vec{n} = \langle 1, 1, 1 \rangle\). The magnitude of this vector is \(\sqrt{3}\), so the unit normal vector is \(\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}} \langle 1, 1, 1 \rangle\). 2. **Parameterize the Surface**: - The plane can be parameterized as \( \mathbf{r}(x, y) = (x, y, 1 - x - y) \) where \(x \geq 0\), \(y \geq 0\), and \(x + y \leq 1\). 3. **Compute the Cross Product**: - The partial derivatives of \(\mathbf{r}\) are \(\mathbf{r}_x = \langle 1, 0, -1 \rangle\) and \(\mathbf{r}_y = \langle 0, 1, -1 \rangle\). - The cross product \(\mathbf{r}_x \times \mathbf{r}_y = \langle 1, 1, 1 \rangle\). 4. **Evaluate the Vector Field on the Surface**: - Substitute \(z = 1 - x - y\) into \(\vec{F}\): \[ \vec{F} = \langle y(1 - x - y), x(1 - x - y), xy \rangle \] 5. **Compute the Dot Product**: - The dot product \(\vec{F} \cdot (\mathbf{r}_x \times \mathbf{r}_y)\) is: \[ y(1 - x - y) + x(1 - x - y) + xy = -x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y \] 6. **Set Up the Double Integral**: - The integral becomes: \[ \iint_D (-x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y) \, dx \, dy \] - The region \(D\) is the triangle with vertices \((0, 0)\), \((1, 0)\), and \((0, 1)\). 7. **Evaluate the Integral**: - Convert to iterated integrals and integrate term by term: \[ \int_{0}^{1} \int_{0}^{1-x} (-x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y) \, dy \, dx \] - After integrating with respect to \(y\) and then \(x\), the result simplifies to \(\frac{1}{8}\). Thus, the flux \( \Pi \) is \(\boxed{\dfrac{1}{8}}\).

The magnitude of this vector is \(\sqrt{3}\), so the unit normal vector is \(\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}} \langle 1, 1, 1 \rangle\). 2. **Parameterize the Surface**: - The plane can be parameterized as \( \mathbf{r}(x, y) = (x, y, 1 - x - y) \) where \(x \geq 0\), \(y \geq 0\), and \(x + y \leq 1\). 3. **Compute the Cross Product**: - The partial derivatives of \(\mathbf{r}\) are \(\mathbf{r}_x = \langle 1, 0, -1 \rangle\) and \(\mathbf{r}_y = \langle 0, 1, -1 \rangle\). - The cross product \(\mathbf{r}_x \times \mathbf{r}_y = \langle 1, 1, 1 \rangle\). 4. **Evaluate the Vector Field on the Surface**: - Substitute \(z = 1 - x - y\) into \(\vec{F}\): \[ \vec{F} = \langle y(1 - x - y), x(1 - x - y), xy \rangle \] 5. **Compute the Dot Product**: - The dot product \(\vec{F} \cdot (\mathbf{r}_x \times \mathbf{r}_y)\) is: \[ y(1 - x - y) + x(1 - x - y) + xy = -x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y \] 6. **Set Up the Double Integral**: - The integral becomes: \[ \iint_D (-x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y) \, dx \, dy \] - The region \(D\) is the triangle with vertices \((0, 0)\), \((1, 0)\), and \((0, 1)\). 7. **Evaluate the Integral**: - Convert to iterated integrals and integrate term by term: \[ \int_{0}^{1} \int_{0}^{1-x} (-x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y) \, dy \, dx \] - After integrating with respect to \(y\) and then \(x\), the result simplifies to \(\frac{1}{8}\). Thus, the flux \( \Pi \) is \(\boxed{\dfrac{1}{8}}\).

The magnitude of this vector is \(\sqrt{3}\), so the unit normal vector is \(\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}} \langle 1, 1, 1 \rangle\). 2. **Parameterize the Surface**: - The plane can be parameterized as \( \mathbf{r}(x, y) = (x, y, 1 - x - y) \) where \(x \geq 0\), \(y \geq 0\), and \(x + y \leq 1\). 3. **Compute the Cross Product**: - The partial derivatives of \(\mathbf{r}\) are \(\mathbf{r}_x = \langle 1, 0, -1 \rangle\) and \(\mathbf{r}_y = \langle 0, 1, -1 \rangle\). - The cross product \(\mathbf{r}_x \times \mathbf{r}_y = \langle 1, 1, 1 \rangle\). 4. **Evaluate the Vector Field on the Surface**: - Substitute \(z = 1 - x - y\) into \(\vec{F}\): \[ \vec{F} = \langle y(1 - x - y), x(1 - x - y), xy \rangle \] 5. **Compute the Dot Product**: - The dot product \(\vec{F} \cdot (\mathbf{r}_x \times \mathbf{r}_y)\) is: \[ y(1 - x - y) + x(1 - x - y) + xy = -x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y \] 6. **Set Up the Double Integral**: - The integral becomes: \[ \iint_D (-x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y) \, dx \, dy \] - The region \(D\) is the triangle with vertices \((0, 0)\), \((1, 0)\), and \((0, 1)\). 7. **Evaluate the Integral**: - Convert to iterated integrals and integrate term by term: \[ \int_{0}^{1} \int_{0}^{1-x} (-x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y) \, dy \, dx \] - After integrating with respect to \(y\) and then \(x\), the result simplifies to \(\frac{1}{8}\). Thus, the flux \( \Pi \) is \(\boxed{\dfrac{1}{8}}\).

The magnitude of this vector is \(\sqrt{3}\), so the unit normal vector is \(\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}} \langle 1, 1, 1 \rangle\). 2. **Parameterize the Surface**: - The plane can be parameterized as \( \mathbf{r}(x, y) = (x, y, 1 - x - y) \) where \(x \geq 0\), \(y \geq 0\), and \(x + y \leq 1\). 3. **Compute the Cross Product**: - The partial derivatives of \(\mathbf{r}\) are \(\mathbf{r}_x = \langle 1, 0, -1 \rangle\) and \(\mathbf{r}_y = \langle 0, 1, -1 \rangle\). - The cross product \(\mathbf{r}_x \times \mathbf{r}_y = \langle 1, 1, 1 \rangle\). 4. **Evaluate the Vector Field on the Surface**: - Substitute \(z = 1 - x - y\) into \(\vec{F}\): \[ \vec{F} = \langle y(1 - x - y), x(1 - x - y), xy \rangle \] 5. **Compute the Dot Product**: - The dot product \(\vec{F} \cdot (\mathbf{r}_x \times \mathbf{r}_y)\) is: \[ y(1 - x - y) + x(1 - x - y) + xy = -x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y \] 6. **Set Up the Double Integral**: - The integral becomes: \[ \iint_D (-x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y) \, dx \, dy \] - The region \(D\) is the triangle with vertices \((0, 0)\), \((1, 0)\), and \((0, 1)\). 7. **Evaluate the Integral**: - Convert to iterated integrals and integrate term by term: \[ \int_{0}^{1} \int_{0}^{1-x} (-x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y) \, dy \, dx \] - After integrating with respect to \(y\) and then \(x\), the result simplifies to \(\frac{1}{8}\). Thus, the flux \( \Pi \) is \(\boxed{\dfrac{1}{8}}\).

The magnitude of this vector is \(\sqrt{3}\), so the unit normal vector is \(\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}} \langle 1, 1, 1 \rangle\). 2. **Parameterize the Surface**: - The plane can be parameterized as \( \mathbf{r}(x, y) = (x, y, 1 - x - y) \) where \(x \geq 0\), \(y \geq 0\), and \(x + y \leq 1\). 3. **Compute the Cross Product**: - The partial derivatives of \(\mathbf{r}\) are \(\mathbf{r}_x = \langle 1, 0, -1 \rangle\) and \(\mathbf{r}_y = \langle 0, 1, -1 \rangle\). - The cross product \(\mathbf{r}_x \times \mathbf{r}_y = \langle 1, 1, 1 \rangle\). 4. **Evaluate the Vector Field on the Surface**: - Substitute \(z = 1 - x - y\) into \(\vec{F}\): \[ \vec{F} = \langle y(1 - x - y), x(1 - x - y), xy \rangle \] 5. **Compute the Dot Product**: - The dot product \(\vec{F} \cdot (\mathbf{r}_x \times \mathbf{r}_y)\) is: \[ y(1 - x - y) + x(1 - x - y) + xy = -x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y \] 6. **Set Up the Double Integral**: - The integral becomes: \[ \iint_D (-x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y) \, dx \, dy \] - The region \(D\) is the triangle with vertices \((0, 0)\), \((1, 0)\), and \((0, 1)\). 7. **Evaluate the Integral**: - Convert to iterated integrals and integrate term by term: \[ \int_{0}^{1} \int_{0}^{1-x} (-x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y) \, dy \, dx \] - After integrating with respect to \(y\) and then \(x\), the result simplifies to \(\frac{1}{8}\). Thus, the flux \( \Pi \) is \(\boxed{\dfrac{1}{8}}\).

The magnitude of this vector is \(\sqrt{3}\), so the unit normal vector is \(\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}} \langle 1, 1, 1 \rangle\). 2. **Parameterize the Surface**: - The plane can be parameterized as \( \mathbf{r}(x, y) = (x, y, 1 - x - y) \) where \(x \geq 0\), \(y \geq 0\), and \(x + y \leq 1\). 3. **Compute the Cross Product**: - The partial derivatives of \(\mathbf{r}\) are \(\mathbf{r}_x = \langle 1, 0, -1 \rangle\) and \(\mathbf{r}_y = \langle 0, 1, -1 \rangle\). - The cross product \(\mathbf{r}_x \times \mathbf{r}_y = \langle 1, 1, 1 \rangle\). 4. **Evaluate the Vector Field on the Surface**: - Substitute \(z = 1 - x - y\) into \(\vec{F}\): \[ \vec{F} = \langle y(1 - x - y), x(1 - x - y), xy \rangle \] 5. **Compute the Dot Product**: - The dot product \(\vec{F} \cdot (\mathbf{r}_x \times \mathbf{r}_y)\) is: \[ y(1 - x - y) + x(1 - x - y) + xy = -x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y \] 6. **Set Up the Double Integral**: - The integral becomes: \[ \iint_D (-x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y) \, dx \, dy \] - The region \(D\) is the triangle with vertices \((0, 0)\), \((1, 0)\), and \((0, 1)\). 7. **Evaluate the Integral**: - Convert to iterated integrals and integrate term by term: \[ \int_{0}^{1} \int_{0}^{1-x} (-x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y) \, dy \, dx \] - After integrating with respect to \(y\) and then \(x\), the result simplifies to \(\frac{1}{8}\). Thus, the flux \( \Pi \) is \(\boxed{\dfrac{1}{8}}\).

The magnitude of this vector is \(\sqrt{3}\), so the unit normal vector is \(\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}} \langle 1, 1, 1 \rangle\). 2. **Parameterize the Surface**: - The plane can be parameterized as \( \mathbf{r}(x, y) = (x, y, 1 - x - y) \) where \(x \geq 0\), \(y \geq 0\), and \(x + y \leq 1\). 3. **Compute the Cross Product**: - The partial derivatives of \(\mathbf{r}\) are \(\mathbf{r}_x = \langle 1, 0, -1 \rangle\) and \(\mathbf{r}_y = \langle 0, 1, -1 \rangle\). - The cross product \(\mathbf{r}_x \times \mathbf{r}_y = \langle 1, 1, 1 \rangle\). 4. **Evaluate the Vector Field on the Surface**: - Substitute \(z = 1 - x - y\) into \(\vec{F}\): \[ \vec{F} = \langle y(1 - x - y), x(1 - x - y), xy \rangle \] 5. **Compute the Dot Product**: - The dot product \(\vec{F} \cdot (\mathbf{r}_x \times \mathbf{r}_y)\) is: \[ y(1 - x - y) + x(1 - x - y) + xy = -x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y \] 6. **Set Up the Double Integral**: - The integral becomes: \[ \iint_D (-x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y) \, dx \, dy \] - The region \(D\) is the triangle with vertices \((0, 0)\), \((1, 0)\), and \((0, 1)\). 7. **Evaluate the Integral**: - Convert to iterated integrals and integrate term by term: \[ \int_{0}^{1} \int_{0}^{1-x} (-x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y) \, dy \, dx \] - After integrating with respect to \(y\) and then \(x\), the result simplifies to \(\frac{1}{8}\). Thus, the flux \( \Pi \) is \(\boxed{\dfrac{1}{8}}\).

The magnitude of this vector is \(\sqrt{3}\), so the unit normal vector is \(\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}} \langle 1, 1, 1 \rangle\). 2. **Parameterize the Surface**: - The plane can be parameterized as \( \mathbf{r}(x, y) = (x, y, 1 - x - y) \) where \(x \geq 0\), \(y \geq 0\), and \(x + y \leq 1\). 3. **Compute the Cross Product**: - The partial derivatives of \(\mathbf{r}\) are \(\mathbf{r}_x = \langle 1, 0, -1 \rangle\) and \(\mathbf{r}_y = \langle 0, 1, -1 \rangle\). - The cross product \(\mathbf{r}_x \times \mathbf{r}_y = \langle 1, 1, 1 \rangle\). 4. **Evaluate the Vector Field on the Surface**: - Substitute \(z = 1 - x - y\) into \(\vec{F}\): \[ \vec{F} = \langle y(1 - x - y), x(1 - x - y), xy \rangle \] 5. **Compute the Dot Product**: - The dot product \(\vec{F} \cdot (\mathbf{r}_x \times \mathbf{r}_y)\) is: \[ y(1 - x - y) + x(1 - x - y) + xy = -x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y \] 6. **Set Up the Double Integral**: - The integral becomes: \[ \iint_D (-x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y) \, dx \, dy \] - The region \(D\) is the triangle with vertices \((0, 0)\), \((1, 0)\), and \((0, 1)\). 7. **Evaluate the Integral**: - Convert to iterated integrals and integrate term by term: \[ \int_{0}^{1} \int_{0}^{1-x} (-x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y) \, dy \, dx \] - After integrating with respect to \(y\) and then \(x\), the result simplifies to \(\frac{1}{8}\). Thus, the flux \( \Pi \) is \(\boxed{\dfrac{1}{8}}\).

The magnitude of this vector is \(\sqrt{3}\), so the unit normal vector is \(\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}} \langle 1, 1, 1 \rangle\). 2. **Parameterize the Surface**: - The plane can be parameterized as \( \mathbf{r}(x, y) = (x, y, 1 - x - y) \) where \(x \geq 0\), \(y \geq 0\), and \(x + y \leq 1\). 3. **Compute the Cross Product**: - The partial derivatives of \(\mathbf{r}\) are \(\mathbf{r}_x = \langle 1, 0, -1 \rangle\) and \(\mathbf{r}_y = \langle 0, 1, -1 \rangle\). - The cross product \(\mathbf{r}_x \times \mathbf{r}_y = \langle 1, 1, 1 \rangle\). 4. **Evaluate the Vector Field on the Surface**: - Substitute \(z = 1 - x - y\) into \(\vec{F}\): \[ \vec{F} = \langle y(1 - x - y), x(1 - x - y), xy \rangle \] 5. **Compute the Dot Product**: - The dot product \(\vec{F} \cdot (\mathbf{r}_x \times \mathbf{r}_y)\) is: \[ y(1 - x - y) + x(1 - x - y) + xy = -x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y \] 6. **Set Up the Double Integral**: - The integral becomes: \[ \iint_D (-x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y) \, dx \, dy \] - The region \(D\) is the triangle with vertices \((0, 0)\), \((1, 0)\), and \((0, 1)\). 7. **Evaluate the Integral**: - Convert to iterated integrals and integrate term by term: \[ \int_{0}^{1} \int_{0}^{1-x} (-x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y) \, dy \, dx \] - After integrating with respect to \(y\) and then \(x\), the result simplifies to \(\frac{1}{8}\). Thus, the flux \( \Pi \) is \(\boxed{\dfrac{1}{8}}\).

The magnitude of this vector is \(\sqrt{3}\), so the unit normal vector is \(\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}} \langle 1, 1, 1 \rangle\). 2. **Parameterize the Surface**: - The plane can be parameterized as \( \mathbf{r}(x, y) = (x, y, 1 - x - y) \) where \(x \geq 0\), \(y \geq 0\), and \(x + y \leq 1\). 3. **Compute the Cross Product**: - The partial derivatives of \(\mathbf{r}\) are \(\mathbf{r}_x = \langle 1, 0, -1 \rangle\) and \(\mathbf{r}_y = \langle 0, 1, -1 \rangle\). - The cross product \(\mathbf{r}_x \times \mathbf{r}_y = \langle 1, 1, 1 \rangle\). 4. **Evaluate the Vector Field on the Surface**: - Substitute \(z = 1 - x - y\) into \(\vec{F}\): \[ \vec{F} = \langle y(1 - x - y), x(1 - x - y), xy \rangle \] 5. **Compute the Dot Product**: - The dot product \(\vec{F} \cdot (\mathbf{r}_x \times \mathbf{r}_y)\) is: \[ y(1 - x - y) + x(1 - x - y) + xy = -x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y \] 6. **Set Up the Double Integral**: - The integral becomes: \[ \iint_D (-x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y) \, dx \, dy \] - The region \(D\) is the triangle with vertices \((0, 0)\), \((1, 0)\), and \((0, 1)\). 7. **Evaluate the Integral**: - Convert to iterated integrals and integrate term by term: \[ \int_{0}^{1} \int_{0}^{1-x} (-x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y) \, dy \, dx \] - After integrating with respect to \(y\) and then \(x\), the result simplifies to \(\frac{1}{8}\). Thus, the flux \( \Pi \) is \(\boxed{\dfrac{1}{8}}\).

The magnitude of this vector is \(\sqrt{3}\), so the unit normal vector is \(\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}} \langle 1, 1, 1 \rangle\). 2. **Parameterize the Surface**: - The plane can be parameterized as \( \mathbf{r}(x, y) = (x, y, 1 - x - y) \) where \(x \geq 0\), \(y \geq 0\), and \(x + y \leq 1\). 3. **Compute the Cross Product**: - The partial derivatives of \(\mathbf{r}\) are \(\mathbf{r}_x = \langle 1, 0, -1 \rangle\) and \(\mathbf{r}_y = \langle 0, 1, -1 \rangle\). - The cross product \(\mathbf{r}_x \times \mathbf{r}_y = \langle 1, 1, 1 \rangle\). 4. **Evaluate the Vector Field on the Surface**: - Substitute \(z = 1 - x - y\) into \(\vec{F}\): \[ \vec{F} = \langle y(1 - x - y), x(1 - x - y), xy \rangle \] 5. **Compute the Dot Product**: - The dot product \(\vec{F} \cdot (\mathbf{r}_x \times \mathbf{r}_y)\) is: \[ y(1 - x - y) + x(1 - x - y) + xy = -x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y \] 6. **Set Up the Double Integral**: - The integral becomes: \[ \iint_D (-x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y) \, dx \, dy \] - The region \(D\) is the triangle with vertices \((0, 0)\), \((1, 0)\), and \((0, 1)\). 7. **Evaluate the Integral**: - Convert to iterated integrals and integrate term by term: \[ \int_{0}^{1} \int_{0}^{1-x} (-x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y) \, dy \, dx \] - After integrating with respect to \(y\) and then \(x\), the result simplifies to \(\frac{1}{8}\). Thus, the flux \( \Pi \) is \(\boxed{\dfrac{1}{8}}\).

The magnitude of this vector is \(\sqrt{3}\), so the unit normal vector is \(\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}} \langle 1, 1, 1 \rangle\). 2. **Parameterize the Surface**: - The plane can be parameterized as \( \mathbf{r}(x, y) = (x, y, 1 - x - y) \) where \(x \geq 0\), \(y \geq 0\), and \(x + y \leq 1\). 3. **Compute the Cross Product**: - The partial derivatives of \(\mathbf{r}\) are \(\mathbf{r}_x = \langle 1, 0, -1 \rangle\) and \(\mathbf{r}_y = \langle 0, 1, -1 \rangle\). - The cross product \(\mathbf{r}_x \times \mathbf{r}_y = \langle 1, 1, 1 \rangle\). 4. **Evaluate the Vector Field on the Surface**: - Substitute \(z = 1 - x - y\) into \(\vec{F}\): \[ \vec{F} = \langle y(1 - x - y), x(1 - x - y), xy \rangle \] 5. **Compute the Dot Product**: - The dot product \(\vec{F} \cdot (\mathbf{r}_x \times \mathbf{r}_y)\) is: \[ y(1 - x - y) + x(1 - x - y) + xy = -x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y \] 6. **Set Up the Double Integral**: - The integral becomes: \[ \iint_D (-x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y) \, dx \, dy \] - The region \(D\) is the triangle with vertices \((0, 0)\), \((1, 0)\), and \((0, 1)\). 7. **Evaluate the Integral**: - Convert to iterated integrals and integrate term by term: \[ \int_{0}^{1} \int_{0}^{1-x} (-x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y) \, dy \, dx \] - After integrating with respect to \(y\) and then \(x\), the result simplifies to \(\frac{1}{8}\). Thus, the flux \( \Pi \) is \(\boxed{\dfrac{1}{8}}\).

Hence, the answer is \boxed{\dfrac{1}{8}}. **Final Answer** The flux \( \Pi \) is \boxed{\dfrac{1}{8}}. </think> To calculate the flux \( \Pi \) of the vector field \(\vec{F} = yz \vec{i} + xz \vec{j} + xy \vec{k}\) through the section of the plane \(x + y + z = 1\) located in the first octant, we follow these steps: 1. **Identify the Surface and Normal Vector**: - The surface is the triangular section of the plane \(x + y + z = 1\) in the first octant. - The normal vector to the plane is \(\vec{n} = \langle 1, 1, 1 \rangle\). The magnitude of this vector is \(\sqrt{3}\), so the unit normal vector is \(\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}} \langle 1, 1, 1 \rangle\). 2. **Parameterize the Surface**: - The plane can be parameterized as \( \mathbf{r}(x, y) = (x, y, 1 - x - y) \) where \(x \geq 0\), \(y \geq 0\), and \(x + y \leq 1\). 3. **Compute the Cross Product**: - The partial derivatives of \(\mathbf{r}\) are \(\mathbf{r}_x = \langle 1, 0, -1 \rangle\) and \(\mathbf{r}_y = \langle 0, 1, -1 \rangle\). - The cross product \(\mathbf{r}_x \times \mathbf{r}_y = \langle 1, 1, 1 \rangle\). 4. **Evaluate the Vector Field on the Surface**: - Substitute \(z = 1 - x - y\) into \(\vec{F}\): \[ \vec{F} = \langle y(1 - x - y), x(1 - x - y), xy \rangle \] 5. **Compute the Dot Product**: - The dot product \(\vec{F} \cdot (\mathbf{r}_x \times \mathbf{r}_y)\) is: \[ y(1 - x - y) + x(1 - x - y) + xy = -x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y \] 6. **Set Up the Double Integral**: - The integral becomes: \[ \iint_D (-x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y) \, dx \, dy \] - The region \(D\) is the triangle with vertices \((0, 0)\), \((1, 0)\), and \((0, 1)\). 7. **Evaluate the Integral**: - Convert to iterated integrals and integrate term by term: \[ \int_{0}^{1} \int_{0}^{1-x} (-x^2 - y^2 - xy + x + y) \, dy \, dx \] - After integrating with respect to \(y\) and then \(x\), the result simplifies to \(\frac{1}{8}\). Thus, the flux \( \Pi \) is \(\boxed{\dfrac{1}{8}}\).

Problem: Find the degree of the splitting field of \((x^5-3)(x^5-7)\) over \(\mathbb{Q}\). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Hmm, splitting fields... right, the splitting field of a polynomial is the smallest field extension where the polynomial factors into linear factors. So, the degree of the splitting field is the dimension of this field as a vector space over \(\mathbb{Q}\). Alright, let me start breaking this down. First, let's look at the polynomial: \((x^5 - 3)(x^5 - 7)\). So, we have two separate polynomials here, \(x^5 - 3\) and \(x^5 - 7\). The roots of \(x^5 - 3\) are the fifth roots of 3, and the roots of \(x^5 - 7\) are the fifth roots of 7. So, to find the splitting field, I need to adjoin all these roots to \(\mathbb{Q}\), right? But splitting fields are generated by all the roots of the polynomial. So, the splitting field here will be the composite field of the splitting fields of \(x^5 - 3\) and \(x^5 - 7\). Let me recall that the splitting field of \(x^5 - a\) (for \(a \in \mathbb{Q}\)) is \(\mathbb{Q}(\zeta_5, \sqrt[5]{a})\), where \(\zeta_5\) is a primitive 5th root of unity. This is because to split \(x^5 - a\), you need both the roots \(\sqrt[5]{a}\zeta_5^k\) for \(k = 0, 1, 2, 3, 4\) and the primitive roots of unity to account for the cyclotomic part. So, the splitting field of \(x^5 - 3\) is \(\mathbb{Q}(\zeta_5, \sqrt[5]{3})\), and the splitting field of \(x^5 - 7\) is \(\mathbb{Q}(\zeta_5, \sqrt[5]{7})\). Therefore, the splitting field of the product \((x^5 - 3)(x^5 - 7)\) would be the compositum of these two fields, which is \(\mathbb{Q}(\zeta_5, \sqrt[5]{3}, \sqrt[5]{7})\). Now, I need to find the degree of this compositum over \(\mathbb{Q}\). To compute the degree, I can use the formula for the degree of a compositum: if \(K\) and \(L\) are finite extensions of \(\mathbb{Q}\), then \([KL : \mathbb{Q}] = [K : \mathbb{Q}][L : \mathbb{Q}] / [K \cap L : \mathbb{Q}]\). So, first, let me figure out the degrees of \(\mathbb{Q}(\zeta_5, \sqrt[5]{3})\) and \(\mathbb{Q}(\zeta_5, \sqrt[5]{7})\) over \(\mathbb{Q}\), and then determine their intersection. Therefore, I think the answer is 100. **Final Answer** \boxed{100} </think> To find the degree of the splitting field of \((x^5 - 3)(x^5 - 7)\) over \(\mathbb{Q}\), we need to consider the splitting fields of each polynomial separately and their compositum. 1. **Splitting Fields of Individual Polynomials**: - The splitting field of \(x^5 - 3\) is \(\mathbb{Q}(\zeta_5, \sqrt[5]{3})\), where \(\zeta_5\) is a primitive 5th root of unity. - The splitting field of \(x^5 - 7\) is \(\mathbb{Q}(\zeta_5, \sqrt[5]{7})\). 2. **Degrees of Extensions**: - The degree of \(\mathbb{Q}(\zeta_5)\) over \(\mathbb{Q}\) is 4, as \(\zeta_5\) satisfies the 4th degree cyclotomic polynomial \(\Phi_5(x)\). - The degree of \(\mathbb{Q}(\zeta_5, \sqrt[5]{3})\) over \(\mathbb{Q}(\zeta_5)\) is 5, since \(x^5 - 3\) is irreducible over \(\mathbb{Q}(\zeta_5)\). - Similarly, the degree of \(\mathbb{Q}(\zeta_5, \sqrt[5]{7})\) over \(\mathbb{Q}(\zeta_5)\) is 5. 3. **Compositum of Fields**: - The compositum of \(\mathbb{Q}(\zeta_5, \sqrt[5]{3})\) and \(\mathbb{Q}(\zeta_5, \sqrt[5]{7})\) is \(\mathbb{Q}(\zeta_5, \sqrt[5]{3}, \sqrt[5]{7})\). - The intersection of \(\mathbb{Q}(\zeta_5, \sqrt[5]{3})\) and \(\mathbb{Q}(\zeta_5, \sqrt[5]{7})\) over \(\mathbb{Q}\) is \(\mathbb{Q}(\zeta_5)\), since the fifth roots of 3 and 7 are independent. 4. **Degree Calculation**: - Using the formula for the degree of a compositum, the degree of \(\mathbb{Q}(\zeta_5, \sqrt[5]{3}, \sqrt[5]{7})\) over \(\mathbb{Q}\) is calculated as follows: \[ [\mathbb{Q}(\zeta_5, \sqrt[5]{3}, \sqrt[5]{7}) : \mathbb{Q}] = [\mathbb{Q}(\zeta_5, \sqrt[5]{3}) : \mathbb{Q}] \cdot [\mathbb{Q}(\zeta_5, \sqrt[5]{7}) : \mathbb{Q}] / [\mathbb{Q}(\zeta_5) : \mathbb{Q}] \] \[ = (4 \cdot 5) \cdot (4 \cdot 5) / 4 = 20 \cdot 20 / 4 = 100 \] Thus, the degree of the splitting field of \((x^5 - 3)(x^5 - 7)\) over \(\mathbb{Q}\) is \(\boxed{100}\).

do_single_rollout

Problem: Let $Y$ be a subset of a metric space $X$. Suppose a subset $E$ of $Y$ can be expressed as $E = Y \cap G$ for some open subset $G$ of $X$. Does this imply that every singleton subset of $E$ is open relative to $Y$? Answer yes or no. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{Yes}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I have this question here: If Y is a subset of a metric space X, and E is a subset of Y such that E can be written as Y intersected with some open set G in X, does that mean every singleton subset of E is open relative to Y? Hmm. Let me think. First, let me recall some topology basics. In a metric space, open sets are defined using the metric, right? And the relative topology on Y (subspace topology) has open sets that are intersections of open sets in X with Y. So if E is Y ∩ G where G is open in X, then E is open in the subspace topology on Y. Got that. Now, the question is asking whether every singleton (a set containing just one point) in E is open relative to Y. So, if I take any point in E, is that point an open set in Y's topology? Well, in general, a singleton set being open depends on the space. For example, in discrete spaces, every singleton is open. But in other spaces, like the real line with the usual topology, singletons are not open; they're closed. But here, we're talking about the subspace topology on Y. So even if Y is a subset of X, the openness of a singleton in Y would require that there's some open set in X whose intersection with Y is just that singleton. So, for a singleton {y} in E to be open in Y, there must be an open set U in X such that U ∩ Y = {y}. But wait, E is already given as Y ∩ G where G is open in X. So E is open in Y. But does that mean every point in E is an open set in Y? Not necessarily. For example, if E itself is open in Y, it doesn't imply that every subset of E, like singletons, are also open in Y. Let me think of a counterexample. Suppose X is the real line with the usual metric, and Y is the interval [0, 1]. Let G be the open interval (0.5, 1.5) in X. Then E = Y ∩ G = (0.5, 1]. This is open in Y because it's the intersection of an open set in X with Y. Now, take a point in E, say 1. Is {1} open in Y? In the subspace topology on Y, a set is open if it's the intersection of an open set in X with Y. To have {1} be open in Y, there needs to be an open set U in X such that U ∩ Y = {1}. But in the real line, any open set containing 1 must include an interval around 1, like (1 - ε, 1 + ε). The intersection of that with Y would be [1 - ε, 1] if ε is small enough. But unless ε is zero, which it can't be, we can't get just {1}. So {1} is not open in Y. Therefore, in this example, E is open in Y, but not every singleton in E is open in Y. Hence, the answer would be no. Alternatively, another example: Let X be the plane, Y be a closed disk, and G be an open disk that overlaps Y. Then E = Y ∩ G would be an open set in Y (a sort of "half-open" disk). But again, individual points in E are not open in Y unless Y has isolated points, which it doesn't in this case. Wait, but maybe if Y is discrete? If Y is a discrete metric space, then every singleton is open. But the question doesn't specify that Y is discrete. It's just a general subset. So the answer must be no in general. But let me check another angle. Suppose that E is open in Y, so in the subspace topology. For every point in E, does there exist an open neighborhood in Y around that point contained in E? Yes, because E is open. But that doesn't mean the singleton itself is open. For example, in the real numbers, the interval (a, b) is open, but no singleton {c} where a < c < b is open. Therefore, being an open set in the subspace topology doesn't make the points within it open; it just means that each point has a neighborhood contained in E. So the answer should be no. The statement does not imply that every singleton in E is open relative to Y. **Final Answer** \boxed{No} </think> Given a metric space \(X\) and a subset \(Y \subseteq X\), consider a subset \(E\) of \(Y\) such that \(E = Y \cap G\) for some open subset \(G\) of \(X\). We need to determine if every singleton subset of \(E\) is open relative to \(Y\). 1. **Subspace Topology**: The set \(E\) is open in the subspace topology on \(Y\) because it is the intersection of an open set \(G\) in \(X\) with \(Y\). 2. **Openness of Singletons**: For a singleton \(\{y\}\) in \(E\) to be open relative to \(Y\), there must exist an open set \(U\) in \(X\) such that \(U \cap Y = \{y\}\). 3. **Counterexample**: Consider \(X = \mathbb{R}\) with the usual topology, \(Y = [0, 1]\), and \(G = (0.5, 1.5)\). Then \(E = Y \cap G = (0.5, 1]\), which is open in \(Y\). However, the singleton \(\{1\}\) is not open in \(Y\) because any open set in \(\mathbb{R}\) containing \(1\) would intersect \(Y\) in an interval, not just the singleton. 4. **General Case**: An open set in the subspace topology does not imply that every singleton within it is open. Openness in the subspace topology means each point has a neighborhood contained in the set, but not that individual points are open. Thus, the answer is \(\boxed{No}\).

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00

Hence, the answer would be no. Alternatively, another example: Let X be the plane, Y be a closed disk, and G be an open disk that overlaps Y. Then E = Y ∩ G would be an open set in Y (a sort of "half-open" disk). But again, individual points in E are not open in Y unless Y has isolated points, which it doesn't in this case. Wait, but maybe if Y is discrete? If Y is a discrete metric space, then every singleton is open. But the question doesn't specify that Y is discrete. It's just a general subset. So the answer must be no in general. But let me check another angle. Suppose that E is open in Y, so in the subspace topology. For every point in E, does there exist an open neighborhood in Y around that point contained in E? Yes, because E is open. But that doesn't mean the singleton itself is open. For example, in the real numbers, the interval (a, b) is open, but no singleton {c} where a < c < b is open. Therefore, being an open set in the subspace topology doesn't make the points within it open; it just means that each point has a neighborhood contained in E. So the answer should be no. The statement does not imply that every singleton in E is open relative to Y. **Final Answer** \boxed{No} </think> Given a metric space \(X\) and a subset \(Y \subseteq X\), consider a subset \(E\) of \(Y\) such that \(E = Y \cap G\) for some open subset \(G\) of \(X\). We need to determine if every singleton subset of \(E\) is open relative to \(Y\). 1. **Subspace Topology**: The set \(E\) is open in the subspace topology on \(Y\) because it is the intersection of an open set \(G\) in \(X\) with \(Y\). 2. **Openness of Singletons**: For a singleton \(\{y\}\) in \(E\) to be open relative to \(Y\), there must exist an open set \(U\) in \(X\) such that \(U \cap Y = \{y\}\). 3. **Counterexample**: Consider \(X = \mathbb{R}\) with the usual topology, \(Y = [0, 1]\), and \(G = (0.5, 1.5)\). Then \(E = Y \cap G = (0.5, 1]\), which is open in \(Y\). However, the singleton \(\{1\}\) is not open in \(Y\) because any open set in \(\mathbb{R}\) containing \(1\) would intersect \(Y\) in an interval, not just the singleton. 4. **General Case**: An open set in the subspace topology does not imply that every singleton within it is open. Openness in the subspace topology means each point has a neighborhood contained in the set, but not that individual points are open. Thus, the answer is \(\boxed{No}\).

Problem: Let $Y$ be a subset of a metric space $X$. Suppose a subset $E$ of $Y$ can be expressed as $E = Y \cap G$ for some open subset $G$ of $X$. Does this imply that every singleton subset of $E$ is open relative to $Y$? Answer yes or no. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{Yes}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I have this question here: If Y is a subset of a metric space X, and E is a subset of Y such that E can be written as Y intersected with some open set G in X, does that mean every singleton subset of E is open relative to Y? Hmm. Let me think. First, let me recall some topology basics. In a metric space, open sets are defined using the metric, right? And the relative topology on Y (subspace topology) has open sets that are intersections of open sets in X with Y. So if E is Y ∩ G where G is open in X, then E is open in the subspace topology on Y. Got that. Now, the question is asking whether every singleton (a set containing just one point) in E is open relative to Y. So, if I take any point in E, is that point an open set in Y's topology? Well, in general, a singleton set being open depends on the space. For example, in discrete spaces, every singleton is open. But in other spaces, like the real line with the usual topology, singletons are not open; they're closed. But here, we're talking about the subspace topology on Y. So even if Y is a subset of X, the openness of a singleton in Y would require that there's some open set in X whose intersection with Y is just that singleton. So, for a singleton {y} in E to be open in Y, there must be an open set U in X such that U ∩ Y = {y}. But wait, E is already given as Y ∩ G where G is open in X. So E is open in Y. But does that mean every point in E is an open set in Y? Not necessarily. For example, if E itself is open in Y, it doesn't imply that every subset of E, like singletons, are also open in Y. Let me think of a counterexample. Suppose X is the real line with the usual metric, and Y is the interval [0, 1]. Let G be the open interval (0.5, 1.5) in X. Then E = Y ∩ G = (0.5, 1]. This is open in Y because it's the intersection of an open set in X with Y. Now, take a point in E, say 1. Is {1} open in Y? In the subspace topology on Y, a set is open if it's the intersection of an open set in X with Y. To have {1} be open in Y, there needs to be an open set U in X such that U ∩ Y = {1}. But in the real line, any open set containing 1 must include an interval around 1, like (1 - ε, 1 + ε). The intersection of that with Y would be [1 - ε, 1] if ε is small enough. But unless ε is zero, which it can't be, we can't get just {1}. So {1} is not open in Y. Therefore, in this example, E is open in Y, but not every singleton in E is open in Y. Hence, the answer would be no. Alternatively, another example: Let X be the plane, Y be a closed disk, and G be an open disk that overlaps Y. Then E = Y ∩ G would be an open set in Y (a sort of "half-open" disk). But again, individual points in E are not open in Y unless Y has isolated points, which it doesn't in this case. Wait, but maybe if Y is discrete? If Y is a discrete metric space, then every singleton is open. But the question doesn't specify that Y is discrete. It's just a general subset. So the answer must be no in general. But let me check another angle. Suppose that E is open in Y, so in the subspace topology. For every point in E, does there exist an open neighborhood in Y around that point contained in E? Yes, because E is open. But that doesn't mean the singleton itself is open. For example, in the real numbers, the interval (a, b) is open, but no singleton {c} where a < c < b is open. Therefore, being an open set in the subspace topology doesn't make the points within it open; it just means that each point has a neighborhood contained in E. So the answer should be no. The statement does not imply that every singleton in E is open relative to Y. **Final Answer** \boxed{No} </think> Given a metric space \(X\) and a subset \(Y \subseteq X\), consider a subset \(E\) of \(Y\) such that \(E = Y \cap G\) for some open subset \(G\) of \(X\). We need to determine if every singleton subset of \(E\) is open relative to \(Y\). 1. **Subspace Topology**: The set \(E\) is open in the subspace topology on \(Y\) because it is the intersection of an open set \(G\) in \(X\) with \(Y\). 2. **Openness of Singletons**: For a singleton \(\{y\}\) in \(E\) to be open relative to \(Y\), there must exist an open set \(U\) in \(X\) such that \(U \cap Y = \{y\}\). 3. **Counterexample**: Consider \(X = \mathbb{R}\) with the usual topology, \(Y = [0, 1]\), and \(G = (0.5, 1.5)\). do_single_rollout

Problem: Let $Y$ be a subset of a metric space $X$. Suppose a subset $E$ of $Y$ can be expressed as $E = Y \cap G$ for some open subset $G$ of $X$. Does this imply that every singleton subset of $E$ is open relative to $Y$? Answer yes or no. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{Yes}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I have this question here: If Y is a subset of a metric space X, and E is a subset of Y such that E can be written as Y intersected with some open set G in X, does that mean every singleton subset of E is open relative to Y? Hmm. Let me think. First, let me recall some topology basics. In a metric space, open sets are defined using the metric, right? And the relative topology on Y (subspace topology) has open sets that are intersections of open sets in X with Y. So if E is Y ∩ G where G is open in X, then E is open in the subspace topology on Y. Got that. Now, the question is asking whether every singleton (a set containing just one point) in E is open relative to Y. So, if I take any point in E, is that point an open set in Y's topology? Well, in general, a singleton set being open depends on the space. For example, in discrete spaces, every singleton is open. But in other spaces, like the real line with the usual topology, singletons are not open; they're closed. But here, we're talking about the subspace topology on Y. So even if Y is a subset of X, the openness of a singleton in Y would require that there's some open set in X whose intersection with Y is just that singleton. So, for a singleton {y} in E to be open in Y, there must be an open set U in X such that U ∩ Y = {y}. But wait, E is already given as Y ∩ G where G is open in X. So E is open in Y. But does that mean every point in E is an open set in Y? Not necessarily. For example, if E itself is open in Y, it doesn't imply that every subset of E, like singletons, are also open in Y. Let me think of a counterexample. Suppose X is the real line with the usual metric, and Y is the interval [0, 1]. Let G be the open interval (0.5, 1.5) in X. Then E = Y ∩ G = (0.5, 1]. This is open in Y because it's the intersection of an open set in X with Y. Now, take a point in E, say 1. Is {1} open in Y? In the subspace topology on Y, a set is open if it's the intersection of an open set in X with Y. To have {1} be open in Y, there needs to be an open set U in X such that U ∩ Y = {1}. But in the real line, any open set containing 1 must include an interval around 1, like (1 - ε, 1 + ε). The intersection of that with Y would be [1 - ε, 1] if ε is small enough. But unless ε is zero, which it can't be, we can't get just {1}. So {1} is not open in Y. Therefore, in this example, E is open in Y, but not every singleton in E is open in Y. Hence, the answer would be no. Alternatively, another example: Let X be the plane, Y be a closed disk, and G be an open disk that overlaps Y. Then E = Y ∩ G would be an open set in Y (a sort of "half-open" disk). But again, individual points in E are not open in Y unless Y has isolated points, which it doesn't in this case. Wait, but maybe if Y is discrete? If Y is a discrete metric space, then every singleton is open. But the question doesn't specify that Y is discrete. It's just a general subset. So the answer must be no in general. But let me check another angle. Suppose that E is open in Y, so in the subspace topology. For every point in E, does there exist an open neighborhood in Y around that point contained in E? Yes, because E is open. But that doesn't mean the singleton itself is open. For example, in the real numbers, the interval (a, b) is open, but no singleton {c} where a < c < b is open. Therefore, being an open set in the subspace topology doesn't make the points within it open; it just means that each point has a neighborhood contained in E. So the answer should be no. The statement does not imply that every singleton in E is open relative to Y. **Final Answer** \boxed{No} </think> Given a metric space \(X\) and a subset \(Y \subseteq X\), consider a subset \(E\) of \(Y\) such that \(E = Y \cap G\) for some open subset \(G\) of \(X\). We need to determine if every singleton subset of \(E\) is open relative to \(Y\). 1. **Subspace Topology**: The set \(E\) is open in the subspace topology on \(Y\) because it is the intersection of an open set \(G\) in \(X\) with \(Y\). 2. **Openness of Singletons**: For a singleton \(\{y\}\) in \(E\) to be open relative to \(Y\), there must exist an open set \(U\) in \(X\) such that \(U \cap Y = \{y\}\). 3. **Counterexample**: Consider \(X = \mathbb{R}\) with the usual topology, \(Y = [0, 1]\), and \(G = (0.5, 1.5)\). Then \(E = Y \cap G = (0.5, 1]\), which is open in \(Y\). However, the singleton \(\{1\}\) is not open in \(Y\) because any open set in \(\mathbb{R}\) containing \(1\) would intersect \(Y\) in an interval, not just the singleton. 4. **General Case**: An open set in the subspace topology does not imply that every singleton within it is open. Openness in the subspace topology means each point has a neighborhood contained in the set, but not that individual points are open. Thus, the answer is \(\boxed{No}\).

In the subspace topology on Y, a set is open if it's the intersection of an open set in X with Y. To have {1} be open in Y, there needs to be an open set U in X such that U ∩ Y = {1}. But in the real line, any open set containing 1 must include an interval around 1, like (1 - ε, 1 + ε). The intersection of that with Y would be [1 - ε, 1] if ε is small enough. But unless ε is zero, which it can't be, we can't get just {1}. So {1} is not open in Y. Therefore, in this example, E is open in Y, but not every singleton in E is open in Y. Hence, the answer would be no. Alternatively, another example: Let X be the plane, Y be a closed disk, and G be an open disk that overlaps Y. Then E = Y ∩ G would be an open set in Y (a sort of "half-open" disk). But again, individual points in E are not open in Y unless Y has isolated points, which it doesn't in this case. Wait, but maybe if Y is discrete? If Y is a discrete metric space, then every singleton is open. But the question doesn't specify that Y is discrete. It's just a general subset. So the answer must be no in general. But let me check another angle. Suppose that E is open in Y, so in the subspace topology. For every point in E, does there exist an open neighborhood in Y around that point contained in E? Yes, because E is open. But that doesn't mean the singleton itself is open. For example, in the real numbers, the interval (a, b) is open, but no singleton {c} where a < c < b is open. Therefore, being an open set in the subspace topology doesn't make the points within it open; it just means that each point has a neighborhood contained in E. So the answer should be no. The statement does not imply that every singleton in E is open relative to Y. **Final Answer** \boxed{No} </think> Given a metric space \(X\) and a subset \(Y \subseteq X\), consider a subset \(E\) of \(Y\) such that \(E = Y \cap G\) for some open subset \(G\) of \(X\). We need to determine if every singleton subset of \(E\) is open relative to \(Y\). 1. **Subspace Topology**: The set \(E\) is open in the subspace topology on \(Y\) because it is the intersection of an open set \(G\) in \(X\) with \(Y\). 2. **Openness of Singletons**: For a singleton \(\{y\}\) in \(E\) to be open relative to \(Y\), there must exist an open set \(U\) in \(X\) such that \(U \cap Y = \{y\}\). 3. **Counterexample**: Consider \(X = \mathbb{R}\) with the usual topology, \(Y = [0, 1]\), and \(G = (0.5, 1.5)\). Then \(E = Y \cap G = (0.5, 1]\), which is open in \(Y\). However, the singleton \(\{1\}\) is not open in \(Y\) because any open set in \(\mathbb{R}\) containing \(1\) would intersect \(Y\) in an interval, not just the singleton. 4. **General Case**: An open set in the subspace topology does not imply that every singleton within it is open. Openness in the subspace topology means each point has a neighborhood contained in the set, but not that individual points are open. Thus, the answer is \(\boxed{No}\).

do_single_rollout

Problem: Every morning when Tim wakes up, he groggily searches around his sock drawer and picks two socks randomly. If he has 10 gray-bottomed socks and 8 white-bottomed socks in his drawer, what is the probability that he picks a matching pair? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{1/3}

Reference Answer: Tim has 18 total socks, so there are $\binom{18}{2} = 153$ ways for him to pick 2 of them. There are $\binom{10}{2} = 45$ ways for him to pick 2 of his 10 gray-bottomed socks, and $\binom{8}{2} = 28$ ways for him to pick 2 of his 8 white-bottom socks, for a total of $45 + 28 = 73$ ways for him to pick a matching pair. So the probability he picks a matching pair is $\boxed{\frac{73}{153}}$.

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00
